White scale bar , 5 µ m . Black scale bar , 1 min . sequentially . Having observed an increased distance between dynamin turns in the co - polymer with endophilin ( Article 2 ) and Farsad et al . ( 2001 ) ) , we wanted to test whether endophilin was also able to insert between turns of a pre - formed dynamin polymer and push them apart . In experiments presented in Article 2 , endophilin had been co - injected with dynamin in the tube - pulling setup . The used lipid composition permitted dynamin to form a limited number of nucleation spots , well spread over the tube , allowing to distinguish areas of the membrane tube coated with dynamin and ones that are not ( 80 % EPC , 19 % Brain PIP 2 , 1 % red ﬂuorescent PIP 2 ; Morlot et al . ( 2012 ) ) . After formation of nuclei on the tube ( Figure 4 . 1A , downfacing arrows ) , endophilin was injected with a second injection pipette . Note the growing polymers when dynamin is injected ( Figure 4 . 1A , four downward facing ar - rows ) . The dynamin polymer did not increase in length upon endophilin binding ( Figure 4 . 1A , left arrow , Figure 4 . 1B , upward facing arrow ) . Dynamin polymerized from several new nucle - ation sites upon endophilin injection ( Figure 4 . 1B , four downfacing arrowheads ) . Moreover , the dynamin intensity increased upon endophilin injection ( Figure 4 . 1A , left arrow ) , probably because dynamin was still in solution at this time point in the experiment in Figure 4 . 1A , and could bind to newly attached endophilin layer . Indeed , this was not observed when dynamin was fully removed before endophilin was injected ( Figure 4 . 1B , upper upward facing arrow ) . The aspect of some dynamin polymers changed upon injection of endophilin + GTP ( upfacing arrow , Figure 4 . 1B ) . Importantly , endophilin bound to existing dynamin polymers only ( Figure 4 . 1A , asterisk ) and not to the membrane tube on its own , it was therefore likely recruited by dynamin . We could not conclude from these experiments that endophilin was able to push apart the turns of a pre - existing dynamin polymer , as no decrease of dynamin ﬂuorescence nor increase of the dynamin polymer length were observed . On the other hand , endophilin did not bind to the membrane in regions without dynamin . Given the low aﬃnity of endophilin for this membrane composition and the absence of a length increase of the dynamin polymer upon endophilin binding , it seemed likely that endophilin did not actually bind to the membrane in the regions where it was recruited by dynamin . Instead , endophilin could have created a second layer on top 4 . 4 Complementary Experiments 73 of dynamin and the recruitement to the tube proximity was solely by the interaction between dynamin’s PRD domain and endophilin’s SH3 domain . It therefore seemed well possible that the protein behaviour of this experiment would be diﬀerent with a membrane composition to which endophilin binds on its own . 4 . 4 . 2 Assessment Of Fission Inhibition Of Endophilin Without Binding To The Membrane In the previous section ( 4 . 4 . 1 ) , the binding of dynamin and endophilin was observed when both proteins were injected sequentially on membrane nanotubes . It had been observed that endophilin bound to pre - formed dynamin polymers without pushing them apart . We asked the question whether binding of endophilin to dynamin , potentially without binding to the mem - brane , was still inhibiting membrane ﬁssion . As we did not have at our disposal an experimental setup with four pipettes ( resulting in three available injection pipettes ) , we designed the fol - lowing experiment , with a membrane tube with the membrane composition from Section 4 . 4 . 1 as a starting point : 1 . Simultaneous injection of 20 µ M endophilin , 5 µ M dynamin and 150 µ M GTP . 2 . a ) Injection of 20 µ M endophilin . b ) Simultaneous injection of 5 µ M dynamin and 150 µ M GTP . If endophilin binding to dynamin without binding to the membrane was not inhibitory for ﬁssion , ﬁssion should occur independently of the endophilin layer . The cumulative ﬁssion probability curve should resemble the obtained from injecting Dynamin + GTP ( Figure 4 . 2A , turquoise – Endo curve ) . In this case we would proceed with 2a ) to show that endophilin was not able to bind to the tube alone . We would do this because the membrane composition of GUVs from the same preparation still varies in exact percentages . If , on the other hand , endophilin binding to dynamin without binding to the membrane was inhibitory , a rather drastic eﬀect was expected , as ﬁssion is completely blocked when using these protein and GTP concentrations on a membrane composition where both proteins clearly bind to the membrane tube on their own ( Article 2 , Figure 2E , F , G ) . In this case , we would proceed with 2b ) to show that the exact same tube was ﬁssion - competent in the absence of endophilin . Figure 4 . 2A shows the outcome of the experiment . The data points were ﬁt to a ( 1 − e − t τ ) using Matlab . Injecting endophilin + dynamin + GTP did not induce ﬁssion ( n = 5 , Figure 4 . 2A , dark red + Endo ( 4x ) curve ) , suggesting that endophilin was inhibitory for dynamin - mediated ﬁssion with this lipid composition as well . As endophilin was not detected to bind to the tube outside the dynamin polymers ( Figure 4 . 1 ) , this was surprising . It suggested that apparently the simple binding of endophilin to dynamin , potentially without direct binding to the membrane , completely blocked ﬁssion . The following injection of dynamin + GTP always induced ﬁssion of these tubes ( Figure 4 . 2A , black dashed pre - Endo curve ) , indicating that the tubes were ﬁssion - competent . Inhibition of ﬁssion by endophilin even without binding to the membrane was surprising . This is why we veriﬁed again that endophilin was not binding to the tube on its own . 20 µ M endophilin + 150 µ M GTP were injected on nanotubes of the same lipid composition . While not having detected endophilin binding to tubes of this composition during the previous experiments on its own , averaging of 10 images after 3 minutes of continuous endophilin + GTP injection now revealed that endophilin was actually binding to nanotubes having this lipid composition ( Figure 4 . 2B , C ) . Binding had not been observed in the previous experiments with the same lipid composition because the binding kinetics were much slower compared to compositions containing PS ( see Article 2 ) . As endophilin was able to slowly bind to the composition used for the experiment presented in Figure 4 . 2 on its own , the ﬁrst hypothesis about ﬁssion inhibition by endophilin without binding to the membrane could not be veriﬁed with this experiment . 4 . 4 . 3 Cloning Of An Endophilin - Dynamin Chimera Plasmid After having observed that endophilin inhibits dynamin - mediated ﬁssion in vitro , we wanted to test inhibition of dynamin - mediated ﬁssion by endophilin in mammalian cells . The idea was to express a chimera protein of endophilin and dynamin in mammalian cells , mimicking a condition in which endophilin is always bound to dynamin . With the help of Carole Seum ( Marcos González - Gaitán lab , University of Geneva ) , a plasmid for mammalian expression was generated using site - directed mutagenesis ( see Figure 4 . 3 ) . The N - terminus of endophilin is an amphipathic helix . In order to not interfere with this helix , the N - BAR domain of endophilin was added to the N terminus of EGFP - Dynamin , resulting in N - BAR - EGFP - Dynamin . The SH3 domain was not included as endophilin and dynamin are linked anyway . In a ﬁrst step , primers of 45 nucleotides ( nt ) were designed where the ﬁrst half was complemen - 4 . 4 Complementary Experiments 75 G F P B A R D y n a m i n pEGFP - C1 pGEX - EndoBAR 45nt primer _ fw megaprimer _ fw mega - primer _ rev Product of 1st PCR E G F P pEGFP - BAR - EGFP - Dyn Product 2nd PCR nick nick 45nt primer _ rev G S T E G F P D y n a m i n B A R 1 2 3 4 Figure 4 . 3 : QuikChange mutagenesis to insert BAR domain into EGFP - dynamin plasmid . tary to the insert , namely the BAR domain of a pGEX - EndoBAR donor vector ( kind gift from Pietro De Camilli , Yale University ) , and the other half to the nucleotides around the site of the receiving vector ( pEGFP - C1 with dynamin inserted at the C terminus of the EGFP ; Pietro de Camilli ) into which the BAR domain was supposed to be inserted ( primer _ fw and primer _ rev in Figure 4 . 3 ) . A PCR with these primers and the donor vector was performed ( 1 ) and the obtained PCR product puriﬁed on an agarose gel ( 2 ) . The single strands of this PCR product now served as megaprimers for a 2 nd PCR with the receiving vector ( 3 ) . The product of this 2 nd PCR is shown in the right of Figure 4 . 3 ( 4 ) . The only diﬀerence to the wanted plasmid are 2 nicks , one in each strand but not at the same position . These nicks are repaired by the bacteria which were transformed with the PCR reaction . As the template of the 2 nd PCR would be selected in these bacteria as well , the solution after the 2 nd PCR is digested with DpnI prior to transformation . DpnI only cuts DNA that is at least hemimethylated . The template is in a methylated state as long as it was produced in the classical dam + dcm + E . coli , while PCR results in demethylated DNA . Therefore , DpnI digestion only digests the template , increasing the probability to obtain clones with the inserted BAR domain after transformation . 4 . 4 . 4 Endophilin - Dynamin Chimera Expression In Mammalian Cells Once the plasmid of the Endophilin - Dynamin Chimera had been obtained , we expressed the chimera in mammalian cells . This experiment was a collaboration with my lab colleague Valentina Galli . HeLa cells were transfected using 0 . 5 – 1 µ g pEGFP - BAR - EGFP - Dyn chimera plasmid ( cloning see Section 4 . 4 . 3 ) or 0 . 5 – 1 µ g human dynamin 1 in pEGFP - C1 ( N - terminal EGFP , kind gift from Pietro de Camilli , Yale University ) with X - tremeGENE HP DNA Trans - fection Reagent ( 06366236001 , Roche Diagnostics GmbH , Mannheim , Germany ) according to the manufacturer’s protocol . Images of the cells were acquired on a microscope ( Nikon Eclipse T1 , Nikon Tokyo , Japan ) with a 100x oil objective ( Nikon CFI Apo TIRF 100x NA 1 . 4 ) , either using total internal reﬂection ﬂuorescence ( TIRF ) or a spinning disk ( 3I , Denver , CO , USA ) . In most of the cells , the EGFP - chimera localised mostly to big aggregate - like structures at the cell membrane which were rarely seen in cells expressing EGFP - dynamin ( Figure 4 . 4A , C ) . Such big structures were present only in very few cells with high expression levels of EGFP - dynamin . While a cytosolic signal of EGFP - dynamin was observed of varying intensities in diﬀerent cells , the cytosolic signal of EGFP - chimera was very low . Also EGFP - dynamin partly localised to punctae , but these were smaller . Considering that the chimera contained a BAR domain 76 Thesis Work : Endophilin Inhibits Dynamin - Mediated Fission A C B D EGFP - Dyn EGFP - Chimera EGFP - Dyn EGFP - Chimera Figure 4 . 4 : Expression of an endophilin - dynamin chimera in HeLa cells . Cells expressing low levels of EGFP - chimera had small punctae that were indistinguishable from those in EGFP - dynamin expressing cells , for their size , localisation and transient nature ( Figure 4 . 4B , D ) . This suggested that the presence of the chimera on structures resembling endocytic pits did not block these , but did not allow to distinguish between abortive events or indeed ﬁssion . This is why we measured transferrin uptake in these cells ( see next section , 4 . 4 . 5 ) . 4 . 4 . 5 Transferrin Uptake In Mammalian Cells Expressing An Endophilin - Dynamin Chimera The observation that the transient small punctae were indistinguishable between EGFP - dynamin expressing cells and EGFP - chimera expressing cells indicated , ﬁrst of all , that the expressed chimera was not blocked at the vesicle neck . At least , several events were dynamic , similar to EGFP - Dynamin overexpressing cells . On the other hand , it was unclear whether the dis - appearance of the punctae reﬂected membrane ﬁssion – leading to a movement of the vesicle and chimera out of the focussed z - plane or disassembly – or to the abortion of the endocytic pit without vesicle formation and ﬁssion . This is why we tested the transferrin uptake in these cells , as the internalisation of transferrin by the transferrin receptor is an indicator for eﬀective CME . This experiment was done in collaboration with my lab colleague Valentina Galli . 4 . 4 Complementary Experiments 77 A B C D E mCherry / EGFP Transferrin Merge NT Dyn - mCherry EGFP - Dyn EGFP - Chimera N T c h e rr y - D y n G F P - D y n G F P - c h i m e r a 0 1000 2000 3000 4000 F l uo r esce n ce i n t e n s i t y ( a . u . ) n . s . n . s . n . s . Experimental procedures : HeLa cells were transfected as described in the previous section ( 4 . 4 . 4 ) , using 0 . 5 µ g of plasmid DNA . As a control , non - transfected HeLa cells and cells transfected with 0 . 5 µ g mouse dynamin 1 in pmCherry - N1 ( C - terminal mCherry ; kind gift from Christien Merriﬁeld , Institute for Integrative Biology of the Cell , Gif sur Yvette ) were used . Cells were incubated with Alexa Fluor 647 - conjugated transferrin ( T23366 , Life Technologies , distributed by ThermoFisher Scientiﬁc , Waltham , USA ) for 15 minutes , washed with PBS and ﬁxed with paraformaldehyde ( PFA ) . Images of the cells were acquired on a confocal microscope ( Nikon Eclipse T1 , Nikon Tokyo , Japan ) with spinning disk ( 3I , Denver , CO , USA ) with a 100x oil objective ( Nikon CFI Apo TIRF 100x NA 1 . 4 ) . z - stack images in a total range of 10 µ m were acquired and maximum projections obtained with ImageJ . The quantiﬁcation of the ﬂuorescence signal was performed in ImageJ by measuring the mean grey value for the transferrin channel ( far red ) per pixel in the area of the cells . Chapter 5 Thesis Work : Collaboration About The Curvature - Dependence Of The Activity Of Vps34 Complex I 5 . 1 Background Of The Collaboration The experiments described in this section are based on a collaboration with Roger Williams and Ksenia Rostislavleva ( Roger Williams lab ) from the MRC Laboratory of Molecular Biol - ogy , Cambridge UK . Roger William’s lab studies the lipid kinase Vps34 that phosphorylates phosphatidylinositol ( PI ) to phosphatidylinositol 3 - phosphate ( PI ( 3 ) P ) . Vps34 is part of several complexes , among which complex I is involved in autophagy and complex II in endocytic sort - ing , autophagy and cytokinesis ( Figure 5 . 1A ) . Ksenia Rostislavleva had observed that complex I and complex II were similarly active on highly curved membrane templates ( small unilamellar vesicles , SUVs ; Figure 5 . 1B ) , while complex I was not active on low - curvature templates ( GUVs ; Figure 5 . 1C ) . They hypothesised that the activity of complex I , which contains Atg14 whose human homologue ATG14L contains a BATS domain that is known to sense curvature ( Fan et al . , 2011 ) , is dependent on curvature while complex II is not . The goal of the collaboration was to test this hypothesis using the nanotube pulling assay . 5 . 2 The Activity Of Vps34 Complex Depends On The Lipid Saturation Status The ﬁrst part consisted in reproducing the microscopy results on the activity of the complexes on GUVs from Rostislavleva et al . ( 2015 ) , using the material at hand in Geneva . Detection of the activity of the complexes occured ( like in Rostislavleva et al . ( 2015 ) ) by using a ﬂuorescently labelled p40PX domain which speciﬁcally binds to the product of Vps34 complex I / II activity , PI ( 3 ) P ( Kanai et al . , 2001 ) . Buﬀer , ATP and protein concentrations were used as described in Rostislavleva et al . ( 2015 ) . To our surprise , complex I was also active in my experiments on GUVs , not only complex II ( Figure 5 . 2A – B ) . The negative control without complexes revealed 80 Thesis Work : Collaboration About The Curvature - Dependence Of The Activity Of Vps34 Complex I DTT ) and then loaded on a pre - equilibrated 5 ml Heparin HP column . The protein was then eluted with an NaCl gradient ( HEP - B buffer , 2 M NaCl , and 0 . 5 mM DTT ) . The kinase - dead vps34 Asp 731 → Asn 731 ( D731N ) dimer was expressed and purified as wild type . Production of nanobodies Nanobodies were generated against cross - linked complex II . Protein for generating nanobodies was produced from 10 liters of yeast cell culture . Complex II was purified as described above , ex - ceptthatthewash2buffer wascomposedof50 mM HEPES at pH 7 . 4 , 300 mM NaCl , 1 mM TCEP , and 0 . 5 mM PMSF . Proteins on IgG beads were incubated overnight with TEV protease in 10 ml of wash 2 buffer at 4°C . After the first elution , 5 ml of wash 2 buffer without TEV protease was added and a second elution was performed . The two TEV elution fractions were combined , and CHAPS was added to a concentration of 0 . 1 % . Protein was concentrated for gel filtration ( Amicon Ultra 100 - kD cutoff , Millipore ) . Gel filtration was carried out on a Superdex20016 / 60 column ( GEHealthcare ) , which was pre - equilibrated with gel - filtration buffer ( 20 mM HEPES at pH 7 . 4 , 300 mM NaCl , and 1 mM TCEP ) . The peak fractions were combined and concentrated to 400 m l at 6 . 3 mg / ml ( 16 m M ) . The whole amount was used for the cross - linking . Complex II at 10 m M was cross - linked using a CovalX K200 stabilization kit ( CovalX , Zurich , Switzerland ) by following the manufacture ’ s proto - col . After removing precipitates by centrifugation , the cross - linked material was frozen in liquid nitrogen and shipped on dry ice to the Steyaert laboratory for immunization . Complex II – specific nanobodies were gener - ated following the previously described protocol ( 15 ) . Scale bar , 10 µ m . 82 Thesis Work : Collaboration About The Curvature - Dependence Of The Activity Of Vps34 Complex I that the PX domain signal on the GUVs incubated with complex I was not stemming from a PI ( 3 ) P contamination in the purchased lipids ( Figure 5 . 2C ) , as no signal was detected on the GUV without complexes . We identiﬁed 5 potential diﬀerences in the experimental protocol . For each diﬀerence , the way it was done in Geneva is indicated ﬁrst : 1 . DOPC / DOPE / DOPS / Soy PI were used instead of Brain PC / Brain PE / Brain PS / Liver PI . 2 . Electroformation of GUVs for 1 h at room temperature instead of 4 h at 60 ◦ C . 3 . Home made chamber instead of Lab - Tek II chamber that is ﬁlled from above . 4 . Passivation of the experimental chamber chamber for 15 min with casein instead of 1 h with BSA . 5 . Mixing of GUVs with protein and buﬀer prior to pipetting whole reaction volume into chamber , instead of adding GUVs into chamber ﬁrst , followed by adding the rest of the reaction and mixing by pipetting up and down . After having tried out the diﬀerent parameters , the decisive factor , which also seemed most likely to us to create the diﬀerence from the beginning , were the precise lipids that were used . Dioleoyl ( DO ) lipids ( DOPC / DOPE / DOPS ) contain two double bonds ( 18 : 1 18 : 1 ) , while the brain - extracted equivalents that were used in Cambridge ( Brain PC / Brain PE / Brain PS ) contain a mixture of fatty acids with 0 to 6 double bonds . In general , using lipids without any double bond ( 16 : 0 16 : 0 or 18 : 0 18 : 0 ) resulted in no activity , while complex I was fully active on lipids with a single double bond ( 16 : 0 18 : 1 ) ( unpublished results from Ksenia Rostislavleva ) . Given that the brain lipids contained more fatty acids without double bonds but at the same time more polyunsaturated fatty acids ( 20 : 4 and 22 : 6 ) , the idea came up that the binding or at least activity of complex I is favoured by lipid packing defects ( Pinot et al . , 2014 ) . Monounsaturated fatty acids contribute to creating rather deep lipid - packing defects which make the lipid headgroups more accessible and resemble curved membranes ( Vamparys et al . , 2013 ) , polyunsaturated fatty acids adapt their conformation and can reduce deep packing defects ( Pinot et al . , 2014 ) . 5 . 3 Testing The Curvature - Dependent Activity Of Vps34 Com - plex I With The Nanotube Pulling Assay The initial starting point for the collaboration was to test the hypothesis that arose from the activity diﬀerence of complex I on SUVs compared to GUVs , by injecting Vps34 complex I on a pulled nanotube from the tube pulling assay and to assess whether the activity was higher on the highly - curved nanotube than on the low - curvature GUV , similar to measuring curvature sensing like in Sorre et al . ( 2012 ) . The diﬃculty of our experiment was though that we did not have ﬂuorescently labelled Vps34 complex I components at hand at this time point which would have allowed to detect curvature - dependent binding of the Vps34 complex I . Instead , we would only be able to detect the product of the reaction that is mediated by the complex , namely PI ( 3 ) P . This would therefore not measure curvature - dependent binding alone but curvature - dependent activity which could partially be the result of curvature - dependent binding . In the case of 5 . 3 Testing The Curvature - Dependent Activity Of Vps34 Complex I With The Nanotube Pulling Assay 83 curvature - dependent activity , we expected to obtain a diﬀusion gradient on the nanotube , similar to Ambroggio et al . ( 2010 ) with ArfGAP1 . While in the experiments from Ambroggio et al . ( 2010 ) , ArfGAP1 activity leads to dissociation of Arf1 from the nanotube , resulting in an Arf1 gradient away from the GUV , we would expect a gradient of the PX signal ( revealing PI ( 3 ) P ) along the nanotube away from the bead towards the GUV , followed by accumulation of PX signal . We did not know whether the kinetics of Vps34 Complex I activity were fast enough to allow to observe a gradient on the nanotube before the generated PI ( 3 ) P diﬀused evenly over the nanotube and GUV . As we were not sure about this , we already had an alternative in mind , namely comparing kinetics of PI ( 3 ) P generation on GUVs with tube compared to GUVs without tube , again expecting faster kinetics in the presence of a nanotube in the case of curvature - dependent Vps34 complex I activity . For the tube pulling experiment , the experimental conditions were adapted as follows : • Adjustment of osmolarities of the glucose inside the GUVs , the solution in the chamber and the protein solution that was injected , by decreasing the glucose concentration inside the GUVs and adding glucose to the two last solutions . • The experimental chamber contained every component of the experiments with GUVs in bulk ( except the mentioned osmolarity adjustment ) , apart from Vps34 complex I . • The injection pipette contained all components from the bulk experiments except GUVs . • 5x increase of the Vps34 complex I protein concentration to 2 . 5 µ M ( injection pipette ) . • Lipid composition in weight % : – 18 % Liver PI – 10 % POPS – 17 % POPE – 40 % DOPC – 15 % DOPS ( ⇒ total PS content : 25 % ) – 0 . 1 % RhodPE – 0 . 03 % DSPE - PEG2000biot The indicated lipid composition was found out experimentally , by varying the amounts of monounsaturated and saturated fatty acid lipids until the speed of activity of Vps34 complex I was in a detectable and convenient range for the nanotube pulling assay . A typical time course of an experiment is shown in Figure 5 . 3A , together with the ROI positions used for the plot in Figure 5 . 3B . Two ROIs were used for the tube , in order to potentially detect a gradient via these means . Two ROIs were also used for the GUV , as the membrane signal on the GUV was diﬀerent in horizontal versus vertical regions . Fits to a dose - response curve function were made using GraphPad Prism ( Figure 5 . 3B ) . The extracted Hill slopes indicated the spead of PI ( 3 ) P generation and the EC 50 value the time point in frames where the maximum PI ( 3 ) P generation occured . The parameters extracted from the data points of Figure 5 . 3B are shown in Figure 5 . 3C , E as examples . PI ( 3 ) P was present on the GUV later than on the tube ( Figure 5 . 3B , C , E ) . Scale bar , 5 µ m . 5 . 3 Testing The Curvature - Dependent Activity Of Vps34 Complex I With The Nanotube Pulling Assay 85 PXtube membranetubePXGUVmembraneGUV ( adapted from Sorre et al . ( 2012 ) ) was calculated , clearly showing again the increase in cur - vature sorting ( due to diﬀerences in generation rates ) around the EC 50 ( Figure 5 . 3D ) . In order to allow to detect a gradient ( if present ) , a longer tube was pulled ( Figure 5 . 3F ) , and indeed a gradient of the PX signal was present on the nanotube . The decrease of the PX signal to the right was not simply caused by the nanotube being out of focus on the right part ( Figure 5 . 3F , membrane channel ) . These results indicated to us that the activity of the Vps34 complex I was higher on the tube than on the GUV . We also measured the activity of Vps34 complex I on GUVs without tube , and also on these GUVs PI ( 3 ) P was generated ( Figure 5 . 3G ) . We compared Hill slopes and EC 50 values of GUVs with vs . without tube . The activity of Vps34 complex I was indistinguishable on GUVs with vs . without tube ( Figure 5 . 3H , I ) . If Vps34 complex I has a curvature dependent activity , then the diﬀerence is not very big , and further experiments would be needed to explore this more in detail . Interestingly , the PI ( 3 ) P generation had a lag phase , as well on GUVs as well as on tubes . The highest PI ( 3 ) P generation ( reﬂected by the EC 50 ) did not occur immediately after the start of injection of Vps34 complex I , but at least 2 minutes later . The observed delay could potentially reﬂect cooperative Vps34 complex I binding or activity . One possibility could be that either Vps34 complex I itself or the PX domain induce curvature , which in turn could either further recruit or activate the Vps34 complex I . Stabilisation of the tube , visible by a drop of the force that the tube applies on the bead and buckling of the tube , had indeed been observed ( Figure 5 . 3A , right ) and also membrane deformations of GUVs ( Figure 5 . 2A , right ) . Chapter 6 General Discussion And Conclusion During my thesis , I used the nanotube pulling assay to study protein properties of the GTPase dynamin , the N - BAR protein endophilin , the clathrin adapter AP180 and the protein complex I of yeast Vps34 . The assessed properties were membrane ﬁssion mediation , ﬁssion regulation , curvature induction and curvature - dependent activity . These experiments are further examples to show how this assay , which was initially developped to test the Canham - Helfrich theory about the membrane elastic energy , can be used to address a wide range of biological questions . The main biological question of this thesis was whether and how the N - BAR protein endophilin regulates dynamin - mediated membrane ﬁssion . The experiments in Chapter 4 provide an - swers to this question . A 4x molar excess of endophilin completely inhibited dynamin - mediated membrane ﬁssion in our nanotube pulling assay . Fission rates and eﬃciencies for diﬀerent endophilin - dynamin molar ratios were measured and we concluded that endophilin does not only delay ﬁssion , like observed for non - hydrolysable GTP analogs ( Morlot et al . , 2012 ) , but also decreases the ﬁssion eﬃciency till complete blockage . Both endophilin’s membrane bind - ing and dynamin binding properties were required for the full blockage of ﬁssion , as shown by experiments using endophilin deletion constructs . A collaboration with Adam Frost and Nathaniel Talledge ( University of California San Francisco ) that involved cryo - EM and class averaging made it possible to obtain the structure of the endophilin - dynamin co - complex on membrane tubes . In the co - complex , 2 – 3 turns of endophilin are inserted between the dy - namin helix turns and the distance between dynamin turns was increased ∼ 2 fold compared to the polymer of dynamin alone . This explained the increased dynamin pitch that is observed on blocked vesicle necks in vivo ( Iversen et al . , 2003 ; Takei et al . , 1998 , 1995 ) . If dynamin also had an increased pitch in the co - complex in the nanotube pulling assay where ﬁssion was blocked , the ﬂuorescence signal of dynamin should be decreased in the co - complex compared to the pure dynamin polymer . The dynamin ﬂuorescence signal was measured for both cases and indeed the ﬂuorescence signal of dynamin was decreased 2 – 3 fold in the co - complex compared to the pure dynamin polymer . This allowed to propose a model for the mechanism by which endophilin inhibits dynamin - mediated membrane ﬁssion ( Figure 6 . 1 ) . While G domains of ad - jacent dynamin helix turns interact in the pure dynamin polymer ( Figure 6 . 1A ) , the endophilin insertion prevents the G domain interactions and the formation of a multi - turn helical dynamin coat ( Figure 6 . 1B ) . Both GTPase activity and constriction are therefore inhibited , which is why 88 General Discussion And Conclusion A B D Dynamin BAR B Interaction Dynamin Membrane tube Membrane tube Figure 6 . 1 : Model for the ﬁssion inhibition mechanism of endophilin . A , G - domains of adjacent dy - namin helix turns interact in the pure dynamin polymer . B , BAR proteins hamper inter - actions between dynamin head groups of adjacent dynamin turns by inserting between the turns , inhibiting constriction and ﬁssion . ﬁssion is blocked in the co - complex . As other BAR proteins also change the architecture of the dynamin coat ( Itoh et al . , 2005 ) , we propose that this mechanism for ﬁssion inhibition also applies to other BAR proteins . If endophilin inhibits dynamin - mediated ﬁssion , the question remains how ﬁssion can then occur in the cell , where endophilin accumulates at the vesicle neck together with dynamin ( Taylor et al . , 2011 ) . Endophilin recruits dynamin to vesicle necks ( Shupliakov , 1997 ; Sundborger et al . , 2011 ; Wigge et al . , 1997 ) , but in this co - complex , dynamin would not be ﬁssion competent . In order to allow ﬁssion , endophilin - free dynamin helix turns would be required . Either endophilin would need to unbind from the co - complex or dynamin would need to polymerise further into additional – endophilin - free – dynamin helix turns on the same membrane tube . For both cases , there are several known possibilities that can be suggested here for the release of the inhibition . The ﬁrst option is to decrease the aﬃnity between the SH3 domain of endophilin and the PRD of dynamin . This could be achieved by an increase of intracellular Ca 2 + levels which is proposed to induce an intramolecular inhibition mechanism where endophilin’s SH3 domain binds to another part of endophilin instead of binding to the PRD of dynamin ( Chen et al . , 2003 ) . Another possibility would be the phosphorylation of dynamin’s PRD ( Chen - Hwang et al . , 2002 ) . It is not clear whether unbinding of the SH3 and PRD domains is enough to release endophilin from the membrane tube but it might be suﬃcient to form additional endophilin - free dynamin helix turns on the same tube . The second option for releasing the inhition is to impede the membrane binding properties of endophilin . This could for example be achieved by LRRK2 phosphorylation of endophilin’s BAR domain ( Matta et al . , 2012 ) . The second question addressed during this thesis was whether the clathrin adapter AP180 induces membrane curvature on its own . A mixture of clathrin and AP180 had been observed to bind to membranes in a membrane tension and rigidity dependent manner and to deform low - tension giant unilamellar vesicles ( GUVs ) at low tension ( Saleem et al . , 2015 ) . In these experiments , AP180 was used as an adapter between clathrin and the membrane because clathrin cannot bind to the membrane itself . In order to distinguish which of the proteins was responsible for the observed membrane property - dependent binding and deformation , my 89 experiments tested whether AP180 contributed to the curvature induction ( Section 3 . 2 . 4 ) . The fact that the force of the tube on the bead in the nanotube pulling assay remained the same , independently of whether AP180 was bound to the tube or not , indicated that AP180 did not induce curvature . Therefore , the observed membrane deformation by the mixture of AP180 and clathrin could be assigned to clathrin or at least a combination of clathrin and AP180 . The third question to which my experiments provided an answer was whether the activity of yeast Vps34 complex I is curvature - dependent ( Chapter 5 ) . This had been suggested by experiments from our collaborators Ksenia Rostislavlea and Roger Williams ( MRC Laboratory of Molecular Biology , Cambridge UK ; Rostislavleva et al . ( 2015 ) ) . The hypothesis of the curvature - dependence of the activity was tested using the nanotube pulling assay . During the replication of results with GUVs in bulk , we discovered that the activity of Vps34 complex I was dependent on the fatty acid content of the lipids of the membrane template in use . The complex was not active on saturated fatty acids ( 16 : 0 or 18 : 0 ) , while it was fully active on lipids containing monounsaturated fatty acids ( 18 : 1 ) . As lipids containing polyunsaturated fatty acids ( 20 : 4 or 22 : 6 ) reduced the activity , we have the hypothesis that the activity of Vps34 complex I is increased with lipid packing defects , which are partially reduced by polyunsaturated fatty acids ( Pinot et al . , 2014 ) . Using the nanotube pulling assay then addressed the initial question of a curvature dependent activity . A phosphatidylinositol - 3 - phosphate ( PI ( 3 ) P ) gradient was detected on the nanotube , which indicated that the activity was higher on the tube than on the GUV because PI ( 3 ) P is the product of Vps34 complex I’s activity . On the other hand , the experiments made clear that if the activity of Vps34 complex I is activated by curvature , the eﬀect cannot be very high , as PI ( 3 ) P was generated with high rates on the less curved GUV membrane as well , similar to those on the nanotube . Further experiments would be needed to explore a curvature - dependent activity more in detail . The last question of this thesis was how mutations in two ubiquitous proteins that are im - plicated in endocytosis result in a hereditary muscle disease . Together with my lab colleague Valentina Galli and my supervisor Aurélien Roux , we addressed this question on a theoretical level in the frame work of a review ( Section 2 . 3 ) . Both dynamin 2 and the N - BAR pro - tein BIN1 / amphiphysin 2 were found to be mutated in patients having the hereditary disease centronuclear myopathy ( CNM ) . For this review , I analysed the structural and functional conse - quences of the mutations in BIN1 , with the currently available published knowledge about the mutations as a starting point . Depending on the domain which was aﬀected by the mutation , either curvature - inducing and scaﬀolding or dynamin binding properties were aﬀected . To - gether with the knowledge about the structural and functional consequences of the mutations in dynamin 2 , we come to the conclusion that both proteins seem to have a muscle - speciﬁc scaﬀolding function in the muscle . T - tubules – tubular invaginations that allow fast signal transmission and actin contraction upon an action potential – are discrupted in CNM patients with dynamin or BIN1 mutations . The scaﬀolding function of both proteins is apparently dis - turbed . Our model proposes that this is due to an imbalance between the two proteins when they are mutated . While dynamin has a major role in membrane ﬁssion in the whole organism , membrane ﬁssion would , according to our hypothesis , need to be inhibited in the muscle , and this is achieved by BIN1 . Dynamin together with BIN1 would stabilise T - tubules in the healthy muscle . One could imagine the same ﬁssion inhibition mechanism that is proposed for en - dophilin in Article 3 in Chapter 4 . To test the hypothesis that BIN1 inhibits dynamin - mediated membrane ﬁssion , one could use the nanotube pulling assay and measure ﬁssion rates for dy - namin together with BIN1 compared to dynamin alone , similar to the experiments presented in Chapter 4 . Nat Cell Biol , 3 ( 10 ) : 922 – 926 . 7 , 8 Appendix Article 3 : A balance between membrane elasticity and poly - merization energy sets the shape of spherical clathrin coats Article published on February 19 th , 2015 in Nature Communications , Volume 6 , Article Number 6249 . Authors : Mohammed Saleem , Sandrine Morlot , Annika Hohendahl , John Manzi , Martin Lenz , Aurélien Roux . A summary of this article is included in Section 3 . 2 . 4 . ARTICLE Received 18 Aug 2014 | Accepted 9 Jan 2015 | Published 19 Feb 2015 A balance between membrane elasticity and polymerization energy sets the shape of spherical clathrin coats Mohammed Saleem 1 , w , Sandrine Morlot 1 , w , Annika Hohendahl 1 , John Manzi 2 , Martin Lenz 3 , * & Aure´lien Roux 1 , 4 , * In endocytosis , scaffolding is one of the mechanisms to create membrane curvature by moulding the membrane into the spherical shape of the clathrin cage . However , the impact of membrane elastic parameters on the assembly and shape of clathrin lattices has never been experimentally evaluated . Here , we show that membrane tension opposes clathrin polymerization . We reconstitute clathrin budding in vitro with giant unilamellar vesicles ( GUVs ) , puriﬁed adaptors and clathrin . By changing the osmotic conditions , we ﬁnd that clathrin coats cause extensive budding of GUVs under low membrane tension while poly - merizing into shallow pits under moderate tension . High tension fully inhibits polymerization . Theoretically , we predict the tension values for which transitions between different clathrin coat shapes occur . We measure the changes in membrane tension during clathrin poly - merization , and use our theoretical framework to estimate the polymerization energy from these data . Our results show that membrane tension controls clathrin - mediated budding by varying the membrane budding energy . NATURE COMMUNICATIONS | 6 : 6249 | DOI : 10 . 1038 / ncomms7249 | www . nature . com / naturecommunications 1 & 2015 Macmillan Publishers Limited . All rights reserved . B udding of spherical vesicles is essential for membrane trafﬁcking . Clathrin was the ﬁrst protein found to be involved in this process 1 , 2 . Clathrin polymerizes into truncated icosahedral cages in vitro 3 and together with various partners 4 deforms the membrane into a spherical bud 5 – 7 . Among the mechanisms involved in bud formation , amphipathic insertions , which act as wedges to create curvature , and the crowding effect 8 of proteins binding asymmetrically to the membrane have been investigated . On the contrary , clathrin was proposed to act by scaffolding , meaning that a protein coat of a given shape forces the membrane to curve 4 . The structure of the smallest clathrin cage is known in great detail 9 – 11 . However , clathrin also forms ﬂat hexagonal lattices at the plasma membrane of cells 12 , and spherical coated vesicles of various sizes , ranging from 35 to 200 nm , in various organisms 13 . This variability in size and shape has called into question the initially proposed membrane scaffolding mechanism based on clathrin polymerization . Clathrin binds membranes through a wide variety of adaptors including adaptin protein complexes ( AP - 1 , AP - 2 , AP - 3 and AP - 4 ) , AP180 / CALM , GGAs and Hrs 14 . Adaptors are speciﬁc to subsets of clathrin - mediated membrane trafﬁc pathways . In particular , AP180 has a high ability to polymerize clathrin in vitro , and forms smaller clathrin cages than clathrin alone 15 . AP180 has an ANTH domain that binds phosphatidylinositol 4 , 5 - bisphosphate ( PIP 2 ) 5 . However , it forms shallow pits when reconstituted on lipid monolayers 5 and does not bud the membrane of large unilamellar vesicles ( LUVs ) 6 . These results suggested that clathrin polymerization energy was not the dominant factor in controlling the shape of clathrin coats . On the contrary , epsin 16 , a clathrin adaptor structurally similar to AP180 , facilitates the clathrin assembly on membranes into highly curved coated buds 17 . Although less potent in clathrin polymerization , epsin has an ENTH domain containing an amphipathic helix - 0 that wedges itself into the outer membrane leaﬂet , helping the build - up of curvature 17 . This supports the direct role of adaptors in curvature generation . Lipid membranes are deformable , ﬂuid surfaces 18 . To deform a membrane by scaffolding , the energetic gain upon coat poly - merization must be at least equal to the energetic cost associated with membrane deformation . The clathrin polymerization energy has never been compared with membrane budding energy . This depends on membrane tension , rigidity and geometry of the bud . We thus questioned how parameters of membrane elasticity ( that are , tension and rigidity ) could affect clathrin polymerization . Results Membrane tension opposes clathrin polymerization . To study the mechanism by which clathrin deforms the membrane , we reconstituted clathrin coats onto giant unilamellar vesicles ( GUVs , 5 – 50 m m ) . We used GUVs containing 30 % 1 , 2 - dioleoyl - sn - glycero - 3 - phosphoserine ( DOPS ) and 10 % PIP 2 ( see Methods ) , mimicking the composition of the inner plasma membrane leaﬂet . The use of 10 % PIP 2 , although higher than physiological concentration , was used to rule out afﬁnity differ - ences between adaptors . Moreover , the physiological concentra - tion of PIP 2 within a clathrin - coated pit remains unknown . GUVs labelled with 1 % of tetra - methyl rhodamine ( TMR ) - PIP 2 were incubated with 0 . 5 m M puriﬁed AP180 and 0 . 4 m M puriﬁed clathrin containing 20 mol % of Alexa Fluor 488 - labelled clathrin ( AF488 - Clathrin ) ( see Methods ) . As expected , clathrin bound to GUVs only in the presence of the AP180 adaptor ( Fig . 1a , b ) and brain PIP 2 ( Supplementary Fig . 1 ) . After clathrin binding , clathrin appeared as a homogenous stain , colocalizing with the membrane signal ( Fig . 1b ) . Clathrin formed a solid coat , as shown by the absence of recovery of coat ﬂuorescence after photobleaching ( Fig . 1c ) . Next , we studied the submicron structure of the clathrin coat using negative - stain electron microscopy ( EM ) of clathrin - coated LUVs . As previously reported 5 , 6 , we observed in most of the cases , small ( a few tens of nm ) , clathrin assemblies ( Figs 1d , e and 2e ; Supplementary Fig . 2 ) , covering the surface of the LUVs . The depth of these clathrin pits was difﬁcult to address by negative stain , although many of them seemed rather shallow ( Fig . 1e ) . Furthermore , the LUVs retained their round shape consistently with our confocal images of non - deformed GUVs . Importantly , all structures observed had limited depth ( see Fig . 1c , and inset of Fig . 2e , isotonic ; Supplementary Fig . 2 ) . As clathrin / AP180 forms highly curved cages in vitro in the absence of membrane 15 , we hypothesized that the combined effect of tension and bending rigidity was responsible for the impairment of full budding by opposing the clathrin polymerization energy . To conﬁrm whether changes in membrane tension affected polymerization of clathrin , we transferred GUVs made in 208 ± 1 mOsm sucrose solution to a clathrin / AP180 solution of varying osmolarity . We examined clathrin polymerization on GUVs under hypotonic ( 180 ± 1 mOsm ) , isotonic ( 208 ± 1 mOsm ) and hypertonic ( 236 ± 1 mOsm ) external buffer conditions . In isotonic conditions , no optically visible deforma - tions of GUVs were observed ( Fig . 1f , h ) . We observed that under hypotonic conditions GUVs did not allow AP180 / clathrin binding in most cases ( Fig . 1f , g ) . We observed that ﬂuorescent AP180 was binding efﬁciently under all osmotic conditions ( Fig . 1i ) with the same density ( see Supplementary Fig . 3 ) , even in the presence of clathrin . Furthermore , the binding of the terminal domain of clathrin ( clathrin TD ) to AP180 did not change signiﬁcantly with the change of osmotic conditions ( Supplementary Fig . 4 ) . We thus concluded that increased membrane tension could totally impair clathrin polymerization . Also , as the surface density of the AP180 did not change in the presence of clathrin ( see Supplementary Fig . 3 ) , and since no deformation of the membrane was observed in presence of 0 . 5 m M AP180 alone in all osmotic conditions , we concluded that protein crowding had only a negligible effect , if any . While the 10 % PIP 2 concentration used provides more than enough binding sites to induce membrane deformation by crowding , the low afﬁnity of AP180 ANTH domain for PIP 2 ( a few micromolars , see ref . 5 ) , and the low AP180 concentration in our assay meant that not all binding sites were occupied . This accounts for the absence of membrane deformation mediated by AP180 crowding . Strikingly , both clathrin and membrane ﬂuorescence signals were strongly increased under hypertonic conditions ( Fig . 1f , g ) . In many cases , long , highly coated membrane tubules emanated from the GUVs , suggesting that under hypertonic conditions , the membrane could be heavily deformed by the clathrin coat ( Fig . 1f ) . We also observed ﬁve times more deformed GUVs in hypertonic conditions than in any other osmotic condition ( Fig . 1h ) . These deformations appeared a few minutes after clathrin addition ( see Supplementary Fig . 5a ) , which was consistent with the time it took to saturate the membrane with clathrin . AF488 - clathrin / AP180 - coated GUVs in isotonic condi - tions did not show more deformation when subjected to hypertonic shock ( Supplementary Fig . 5b ) . These results support our hypothesis that GUV deformation was a result of clathrin polymerization . To better characterize the shape of the membrane deforma - tions , GUVs incubated with AP180 / clathrin under varying osmotic conditions were ﬁxed and processed for thin - section electron microscopy ( TEM , see methods ) . Similar to the confocal images , TEM also revealed uncoated membranes when GUVs were incubated with proteins under hypotonic conditions ( Fig . 2a , d ) . GUVs incubated under isotonic conditions revealed ARTICLE NATURE COMMUNICATIONS | DOI : 10 . 1038 / ncomms7249 2 NATURE COMMUNICATIONS | 6 : 6249 | DOI : 10 . 1038 / ncomms7249 | www . nature . com / naturecommunications & 2015 Macmillan Publishers Limited . All rights reserved . an electron - dense coat that did not cause observable deformations to the membrane ( Fig . 2b , d ) . Furthermore , LUVs incubated with AP180 / clathrin under isotonic conditions ( see Fig . 2e ) remained round shaped , as seen by negative - stain EM . On these images ( see also Supplementary Fig . 2 ) the surface of the LUVs was covered with limited assemblies of clathrin , conﬁrming that the electron - dense coat seen by TEM was indeed a proper clathrin coat . However , these images show that rather than a purely hexagonal ﬂat lattice , the clathrin coat was constituted of densely packed shallow buds on the membrane ( see Fig . 1d , e and Supplementary Fig . 2 , monolayer and isotonic ) . Finally , GUVs incubated under hypertonic conditions showed an entirely budded surface , covered with an electron - dense coat ( Fig . 2c , d ) . Coated membrane buds were observed with an average diameter of 65 ± 5 nm , close to values of in vitro formed AP180 / clathrin cages 15 . In summary , observations made by EM ( both TEM and negative stain ) were consistent with our confocal microscopy data ( Fig . 1 ) , and conﬁrmed that AP180 and clathrin can deform the membrane in a membrane tension - dependent way . Membrane elastic energy against clathrin polymerization energy . To quantitatively test our hypothesis , we developed a theoretical model that describes the competition between clathrin polymerization , which favours full budding , membrane tension and rigidity , which favour planar membranes , to predict transi - tions between different possible bud shapes ( see Fig . 3a ) . In the model , a ﬂat membrane with tension s , and bending rigidity k , is in contact with a clathrin / AP180 solution at ﬁxed concentrations . We denoted by m the free energy gain associated with the poly - merization of a unit area of clathrin coat . As membrane - bound clathrin coats are very rigid ( bending rigidity k c C 300 k B T 19 ) , we assumed that clathrin always polymerizes into spheres of ﬁxed radius r c . In our model , tension cannot modify the coat’s radius , but it can limit clathrin polymerization to partial buds as illustrated in Fig . 3a , b . Accordingly , our model considers four Hypotonic Hypertonic Isotonic Thin / uncoated Thick coat Deformed 100 80 60 40 20 0 P e r c en t age v e s i c l e ( % ) Hypotonic Isotonic Hypertonic Isotonic 200 nm 200 nm 100 nm 100 n m Hypertonic Figure 2 | TEM images of GUVs incubated with AP180 / clathrin under different osmotic conditions . ARTICLE NATURE COMMUNICATIONS | DOI : 10 . 1038 / ncomms7249 4 NATURE COMMUNICATIONS | 6 : 6249 | DOI : 10 . 1038 / ncomms7249 | www . nature . com / naturecommunications & 2015 Macmillan Publishers Limited . All rights reserved . possible bud states illustrated in Fig . 3a : ( 1 ) bare membrane : clathrin cannot polymerize , and the membrane remains uncoated ; ( 2 ) shallow partial bud : clathrin polymerizes partially into buds that are less than a half sphere ; ( 3 ) deep partial bud : clathrin polymerizes into a bud that is more than a half sphere but less than a full sphere and ( 4 ) full bud : a full spherical clathrin coat forms around the membrane bud . This coat is traversed by a small membrane tether of negligible energy 20 that connects the membrane bud to the main membrane . This connection is the only difference between our full buds and free clathrin - coated vesicles . This description is consistent with the partial coats observed by negative - stain EM ( Fig . 2e ; Supplementary Fig . 2 ) . To support this geometrical picture , we measured the average size of partial buds grown on mica - supported bilayers by atomic force microscopy to ﬁnd a height of 15 – 20 nm , and a diameter of 80 – 100 nm ( see Supplementary Fig . 6 ) . Subtracting the B 15 nm thickness of the clathrin coat , these measurements are consistent with membrane buds of B 30 nm radius , and a depth of a few nanometres as illustrated in Fig . 3b . This model is also consistent with our EM studies , where we observed bare membranes in hypotonic conditions ( Fig . 2a ) , shallow buds in isotonic conditions ( Fig . 2a , e ) and deep buds in hypertonic conditions ( Fig . 2a , f ) . These observations support our assumption that AP180 / clathrin mostly polymerizes with a roughly constant radius of curvature r c . From our TEM pictures in hypertonic conditions ( see above and Fig . 2 ) , we thus concluded that r c ¼ 32 . 5 nm . The clathrin polymerization energy was measured from 15 independent experiments . All rights reserved . ( positive ) clathrin - binding free energy and (cid:2) k = 2 r 2c (cid:2) (cid:3) , the cost of bending the membrane into a bud . In addition , we introduced the coat’s line tension t , describing the energetic cost of broken clathrin – clathrin bonds at the boundary of a partial bud . This leads to the following expression for the free energy of a single bud : f ¼ s A m (cid:2) m A c þ t L ð 1 Þ where A m and A c are the membrane and coat areas , respectively , associated with one bud , and L is the length of its rim ( Fig . 3a ) . In this expression , m is dimensionally equivalent to a force per unit length , and represents the net force that a clathrin coat applies on the membrane while polymerizing . The membrane tension s and the bending rigidity k oppose this force , while the coat’s line tension t favours buds with short rims . To make predictions at the liposome level , we applied this theoretical framework to a densely packed assembly of coated buds ( see Fig . 3b and Supplementary Discussion ) . Depending on the value of the parameters , we can analytically predict the shape of the clathrin buds ( bare membrane , shallow / deep partial buds or full buds ) as shown in the phase diagram Fig . 3c . Qualitatively , for low clathrin polymerization energies ( m r 2 t / r c ) , the membrane is either bare for high tensions ( s 4 m ) or fully budded if s r m . These two regimes still exist at high polymerization energies ( m 4 2 t / r c ) , but with the added possibility of partial buds at moderate tensions . This high polymerization energy situation reproduces our above observations of GUVs under various osmotic conditions . We observed no polymerization for high tensions , and full budding for low tensions . Moreover , we do not observe purely hexagonal lattices ( see Fig . 2 , and also Supplementary Fig . 2 ) , and thus interpret the continuous ﬂuorescent staining of clathrin on GUVs ( Fig . 1 ) in isotonic conditions as a dense coverage of contiguous shallow buds ( see Figs 1e and 3b ) . To validate our model , we measured membrane tension s and clathrin polymerization energy m . We used an in vitro micro - manipulation assay ( see Fig . 3d ) 21 to pull a membrane nanotube out of GUVs by means of a streptavidin bead held in optical tweezers . We modulated the membrane tension s by aspirating the GUV in a micropipette . This technique allowed us to quantify and control tension in a way that cannot be achieved by osmotic shock . We measured the force F required to maintain the tube using optical tweezers . In the absence of clathrin , this force is given by 22 F ¼ 2 p ﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃﬃ 2 ks p , allowing us to measure 23 the membrane bending modulus k ¼ 13 ± 5 k B T . Once the tube was formed , a mix of AP180 and AF488 - clathrin in isotonic buffer was injected using a second micropipette ( see Methods and Fig . 3d ) . Clathrin binding was observed along the membrane tube and on the GUV surface . Clathrin GUV staining was homogeneous ( Fig . 3e ) , consistent with the formation of a continuous ﬂat coat as in Fig . 1a , b . Following clathrin polymerization , we observed a reduction of the total apparent GUV membrane area in 87 % of all experiments , consistent with the sequestration of the membrane into clathrin - coated pits ( see Fig . 3d , f ; Supplementary Table 1 ) . The 6 % reduction of the apparent GUV area ( see Supplementary Table 1 ) allowed us to calculate an average bud depth of 2 nm , which is consistent with our EM data . During clathrin polymerization on the GUV and the tube , we measured a drop in the tube force ( see Fig . 3f ) . AP180 alone did not induce a signiﬁcant force drop when binding to the tubes ( see Supplementary Fig . 7 ) . In cases where the apparent membrane area decreased , we used our mathematical model to infer the values of the parameters m and t from the decrease in apparent membrane area and tube force ( Supplementary Discussion ) . The resulting values are given in Supplementary Table 1 and denoted by full circles in the phase diagram of Fig . 3g ( colour code identical to Fig . 3c ) . In all cases , the measured values of m and s fell in the shallow bud regime , conﬁrming our hypothesis that continuous staining of clathrin - coated GUVs in isotonic conditions were indeed contiguous shallow buds . The measured value of the clathrin polymerization energy per unit area was equal to 7 . 6 ± 5 . 4 (cid:3) 10 (cid:2) 5 N . m (cid:2) 1 , with m 0 ¼ 1 . 0 ± 0 . 5 (cid:3) 10 (cid:2) 4 N . m (cid:2) 1 . We found t ¼ 5 . 2 ± 7 . 4 (cid:3) 10 (cid:2) 2 pN , well below values at which line tension is able to bud membrane domains on its own ( typically 1 pN , see ref . 24 ) . This supports our hypothesis that membrane bending is primarily due to clathrin polymerization and not due to the line tension . The clathrin polymerization energy is the sum of two contributions : clathrin – clathrin interactions and clathrin – membrane interactions mediated by the adaptors , which we express as m 0 ¼ m c (cid:2) c þ m c (cid:2) m . In the scaffolding mechanism , clathrin polymerization is the main driver of membrane deformation , implying that m c (cid:2) c is much larger than m c (cid:2) m . To test this model , we estimated the relative importance of m c (cid:2) c and m c (cid:2) m by measuring the force required to rupture the clathrin coat . We aspirated clathrin - coated GUV using a micropipette of radius R p C 1 m m ( Fig . 4a ) in isotonic conditions . Upon increasing the aspiration pressure above D P c ¼ 231 ± 26 Pa , we observed a sudden rupture of the coat along the rim of the pipette followed by marked membrane tongue elongation ( Fig . 4a , b ) . The resulting cracks within the coat were visible both during a prolonged aspirated state and after releasing the aspiration ( Fig . 4c ) . We reasoned that rupturing the coat in this way disrupted the clathrin – clathrin bonds while leaving the clathrin – membrane interactions unaffected , thus allowing us to estimate m c (cid:2) c independently of m c (cid:2) m . From D P c and R p , we estimated m c (cid:2) c ¼ 1 . 1 ± 0 . 1 (cid:3) 10 (cid:2) 4 N . m (cid:2) 1 ( see Supplementary Note 1 ) . This value is indistinguishable from m 0 ¼ 1 . 0 ± 0 . 5 (cid:3) 10 (cid:2) 4 N . m (cid:2) 1 , conﬁrming that clathrin – clathrin interactions are the predomi - nant interactions responsible for clathrin coat’s assembly as in the scaffolding model . Multiplying m c (cid:2) c by the speciﬁc area a C 800nm 2 occupied by a triskelion ( see Supplementary Note 2 ) yields the binding energy per triskelion a m c (cid:2) c ¼ 23 ± 3 k B T . Our value of the binding energy is consistent with a previous theoretical estimate based on the critical concentration of clathrin polymerization 25 , a m c (cid:2) c ¼ 42 k B T , and with estimates coming from computational assembly statistics 26 , where a m c (cid:2) c ¼ 23 k B T . Also , it is too large to allow for internal rearrangements of the clathrin lattice once polymerized , implying that clathrin buds directly polymerize into curved structures rather than going through a hexagonal ﬂat coat intermediate . Espin facilitates clathrin polymerization . To further validate our model , we used these results to predict the shape of clathrin coats by changing parameters . We tested this prediction experimentally by investigating the predicted role of the bending rigidity in inhibiting budding . The expression m ¼ m 0 (cid:2) k = 2 r 2c (cid:2) (cid:3) implies that an increased k should reduce the value of the polymerization energy m . All rights reserved . adding sphingolipids and cholesterol ( 60 % brain sphingomyelin ( BSM ) , 30 % DOPS , 10 % PIP 2 , with 50 % cholesterol ) to increase the bending rigidity to k ¼ 51 ± 20 k B T . This decreased the average value of m by more than an order of magnitude ( m stiff ¼ 3 . 7 (cid:3) 10 (cid:2) 6 N . m (cid:2) 1 ) , implying a marked decrease of the maximum tension allowing clathrin assembly to 3 . 5 (cid:3) 10 (cid:2) 6 N . m (cid:2) 1 . Consistent with our expectation , clathrin failed to bind to a vast majority of liposomes ( Fig . 5a ) under isotonic conditions despite signiﬁcant binding of AP180 ( Fig . 5d ) . We further tested whether clathrin polymerization could be restored by changing membrane tension , but did not observe any clathrin binding neither under hypotonic nor hypertonic conditions ( see Fig . 5b , c ) , further showing the inﬂuence of membrane bending rigidity on clathrin polymerization . To further test our model , we predicted that any adaptors with membrane - deforming properties should assist budding . We thus studied the polymerization of clathrin on GUVs as mediated by epsin . We found that epsin binds to liposomes under all tested membrane tensions ( Supplementary Fig . 8 ) without causing observable deformations , as does AP180 . When GUVs were incubated with the unlabelled epsin and AF488 - clathrin under hypotonic conditions , 70 % of the vesicles showed membrane deformations ( Fig . 5e , f ) . Signiﬁcant membrane deformation and tubulation were also observed under isotonic conditions . Like - wise , under hypertonic conditions the GUVs were highly deformed ( Fig . 5d ) . Thus the membrane - deforming properties of epsin facilitated clathrin polymerization to such an extent that it was able to bind to GUVs even under unfavourable membrane tension conditions . Discussion In this study , we showed that the shape of clathrin coats is controlled by membrane tension . This mechanism is based on counteracting clathrin polymerization by membrane tension , which hinders the closure of the clathrin - coated pits : at low membrane tension , deep buds can form , whereas intermediate or high tensions will only result in partial budding . We measured the clathrin polymerization energy and found values that are in the range of in vivo membrane tension values ( 10 (cid:2) 5 – 10 (cid:2) 4 N . m (cid:2) 1 —see refs 27 – 29 ) . Thus , in vivo values of membrane tension cover the shape transition from a shallow to a deep budded coat , supporting a physiological control of clathrin budding through membrane tension . It is consistent with the fact that clathrin - mediated endocytosis is delayed in cells that have higher membrane tension and require actin for completion 30 , 31 . We imagine that at the structural level , membrane tension does not allow for the correct binding angle between arms of the triskelia , impairing their correct association . We also found that increased membrane rigidity opposes clathrin budding . It is consistent with the fact that clathrin - mediated endocytosis is slower at the apical pole of epithelia , where the membrane is more rigid than at the basal pole 32 . We also found that the membrane - deforming ability of epsin favours clathrin - mediated budding , even when the membrane’s elastic parameters ( namely tension and rigidity ) are not favourable . This indicates that the different actors that can generate membrane curvature ( wedge insertion , crowding or local forces exerted by the cytoskeleton ) can act synergistically to deform the membrane : the role of adaptors and actin in clathrin - induced membrane deformation has been studied extensively . However , conclusions about whether they are required or sufﬁcient for membrane deformation proved themselves difﬁcult to generalize to all biological systems . Our results support a modulatory role of each adaptor and actin depending on the difﬁculty of deforming the membrane 31 : in an extreme case where the membrane is very stiff and tension is high , all partners are required , whereas they become dispensable in intermediate conditions . For energetic 25 , kinetic and biochemical reasons 33 , it seemed more favourable to consider that clathrin polymerizes directly into its ﬁnal curved shape . However , explaining the various shape of clathrin coats has for long been a matter of debate : it was proposed 12 and analysed theoretically 26 , 34 how the clathrin lattice could be reorganized to change shape once polymerized . Another possibility is that the clathrin coat is soft enough once polymerized to deform elastically under increasing tension . However , measurement of the clathrin - coated membrane bending rigidity showed it is about 10 times more than free lipid membranes , which accounts for less than 5 % change in size by elastic deformation ( theory not shown ) . Moreover , our model and ﬁndings favour the direct polymerization of clathrin in a curved lattice with AP180 . They also account for how the shape of the bud could be modulated by membrane tension . Our ﬁndings are compatible with the fact that partial knockdown of clathrin generates shallow pits in vivo 35 , as our model predicts a decrease in clathrin’s cytosolic concentration would result in a lowering of its polymerization energy m . Similar shallow coats were also observed with both H 6 - D ANTH - AP180 and full - length H 6 - AP180 , together with clathrin in a recent reconstitution study suggesting that clathrin polymerization alone could drive membrane bending 13 . The polymerization of other membrane coats has also been shown to depend on tension . Such examples include the shape of Coat Proteins I ( COPI ) coats that change from ﬂat to a budded state when tension is decreased 36 , and caveolae that can be disrupted by increased membrane tension 28 . More generally , actin plays a critical role in the clathrin bud formation , in all cell types that have been studied , from yeast 37 to mammals 28 , 29 . Actin is the main membrane tension regulator in eukaryotic cells . Its role in clathrin - mediated endocytosis thus may indicate that membrane tension is a general regulator of clathrin - mediated endocytosis . Two lipid compositions were used— ( 1 ) 25 % DOPC þ 30 % DOPE þ 5 % liver PI þ 30 % DOPS þ 10 % PIP 2 supplemented with 15 % cholesterol and 1 % TMR - PIP 2 ; ( 2 ) 60 % BSM þ 30 % DOPS þ 10 % PIP 2 þ 1 % TMR - PIP 2 supplemented with 50 % cholesterol . streptavidin - coated polystyrene beads ( 3 . 05 m m ) used for nanotube pulling experiments were purchased from Spherotech . Clathrin was extracted from fresh porcine brains and puriﬁed as described in ref . 15 . Clathrin was labelled with Alexa Fluor 488 C5 maleimide ( Invitrogen ) . AP180 was expressed in BL21 / DH3 a cells and puriﬁed by glutathione S transferase pull - down and glutathione S transferase cleaved by PreScission protease . AP180 was labelled with Alexa Fluor 488 C5 maleimide ( Invitrogen ) . Epsin was puriﬁed from cells infected with baculovirus as described in ref . 5 and labelled with Alexa Fluor 488 C5 maleimide ( Invitrogen ) . All proteins were aliquoted in GTPase buffer ( HEPES 20m M pH ¼ 7 . 4 , NaCl 100m M and MgCl 2 1m M ) and stored at (cid:2) 80 (cid:2) C . Preparation of GUVs . The GUVs were composed of 25 % DOPC , 30 % DOPE , 5 % liver PI , 30 % DOPS , 10 % PIP 2 , 15 % cholesterol and 1 % TMR - PIP 2 ( ﬂuorescent lipid ) in chloroform / methanol at a concentration of 2mgml (cid:2) 1 . The lipid mix was dried under N 2 ﬂux and further dried under vacuum for 1h before being resuspended in pure chloroform . To obtain a good yield of GUVs containing highly negatively charged lipids , the electroformation of GUVs was performed as described in ref . 21 . Brieﬂy , a uniform smear of 10 m l of lipid mix at 2mgml (cid:2) 1 was ﬁrst dried on conductive indium - tin oxide - coated glass ( Pra¨zisions Glas and Optik GmbH ) for 30min at 60 (cid:2) C , followed by drying under vacuum for at least 1h . The lipid ﬁlm was then rehydrated in a sucrose solution at 208 ± 1mOsm and GUVs were allowed to grow for 1h under a sine voltage ( 900mV , 10Hz ) . For iso - osmotic experimental conditions , a sucrose solution of equal osmolarity as the protein solution was used , while for hypertonic and hypotonic conditions , more concentrated or less concentrated ( respectively ) sucrose solutions were used . Imaging was carried out on a Nikon confocal microscope . Identical laser power and gain settings were used during the course of all experiments . Electron microscopy . An excess of GUVs in different osmotic environments was incubated with protein mix and visualized by confocal ﬂuorescence microscopy for binding of AP180 / clathrin . The protein - bound GUVs were ﬁxed with 4 % glutar - aldehyde , embedded in 2 % agar to increase the sedimentation of the liposomes and spun for 5 , 000r . p . m . for 3 – 4min to pellet down the sample . The sample was treated with 0 . 4 M Millonig’s buffer ( sodium phosphate ( monobasic ) þ NaOH ) containing 2 % osmium tetroxide for 1h and rinsed with ddH 2 O . The sample was stained with 0 . 25 % uranyl acetate overnight and rinsed with ddH 2 O followed by sequential dehydration in 30 , 50 , 70 and 90 % ethanol for 5 – 10min . The last dehydration step was carried out three times in absolute ethanol for 30min each . The sample was then washed with propylene oxide twice for 10min followed by incubation in epon - propylene oxide ( 1 : 1 ) for 1h . Finally , the sample was treated with 100 % epon overnight at room temperature before being embedded in 1ml of epon resin mix followed by curing at 65 (cid:2) C for at least 24h . Ultrathin sectioning was performed using a microtome ( Leica Ultracut ) at a cutting angle of 6 (cid:2) . Sections were put on glow - discharged carbon - coated formvar grids and viewed with a Tecnai G2 Sphera ( FEI ) electron microscope at an acceleration voltage of 160kV and an exposure time of 1 , 000ms . Also , for negative - stain EM observations , LUVs were ﬁrst incubated with AP180 / clathrin in suspension for 10 – 15min and then adsorbed onto Formvar - coated EM grids . The samples were ﬁxed and negatively stained with 2 % uranyl acetate before visualization . Clathrin polymerization energy measurements . GUVs were aspirated in a micropipette controlled by a motorized micromanipulator ( Sutter MP225 ) and a custom - made hydraulic system to control the aspiration pressure D P and to set the membrane tension : s ¼ 12 R p D P 1 (cid:2) R p R v where R p and R v are the radii of the pipette and GUV , respectively . A membrane nanotube was pulled out of a GUV whose membrane was attached to a streptavidin - coated bead ( 3 . 05 m m diameter , Spher - otec ) held in a ﬁxed optical trap . The optical trap was custom - made with an ytterbium ﬁbre laser focused through a 100 (cid:3) 1 . 3 numerical apperture oil immersion objective . The force F exerted on the bead was calculated from the Hooke’s law : F ¼ k * D x , where k is the stiffness of the trap ( k ¼ 360pN m m (cid:2) 1 W (cid:2) 1 ) calibrated by the viscous drag method 38 and D x the displacement of the bead from its equilibrium position in the optical trap measured with a custom - made video tracking software . AP180 and clathrin were injected close to the nanotube using a second micropipette with a typical radius of 10 m m controlled with a hydraulic micromanipulator ( Narishige ) . Nanotubes were visualized simultaneously by brightﬁeld imaging ( Pixelink camera ) and by dual - colour confocal microscopy ( l 1 ¼ 488nm and l 2 ¼ 543nm ) on an inverted Nikon eclipse Ti microscope . The clathrin polymerization energy was measured for increasing values of set membrane tensions . Image analysis . Images were analysed and processed with ImageJ . The ﬂuorescence signal , F vesicle along the equatorial plane of GUVs was quantiﬁed by measuring the average ﬂuorescence of the membrane using the OvalProﬁle plugin of ImageJ . The background ﬂuorescence of the unbound protein , F background was measured in an area close to the vesicle . Fluorescence intensity of the vesicle was denoted as I ¼ ( F vesicle (cid:2) F background ) . Same illumination and detection settings were used for all the experimental conditions . Electron micrographs were manually analysed using ImageJ to measure the average bud diameter of 65 ± 5nm under hypertonic conditions . Atomic force microscopy on supported bilayer . Ten m l of a 10 - mgml (cid:2) 1 lipid mixture was dropped onto a circular glass coverslip , air dried and kept under vacuum for at least 1h . The dried lipid smear was hydrated with 90 m l of GTPase buffer for 30min , followed by rinsing to peel off the membrane regions that did not adhere to the surface . Two m l of the protein mix ( AP180 þ clathrin ) was incubated with the adhered membrane sheet for 30min , followed by rinsing with GTPase buffer to wash the unbound protein . The protein - binding membrane sheet was then ﬁxed with 4 % glutaraldehyde and 1 . 8 % osmium tetroxide for 1h , followed by washes with GTPase buffer . The sample was then dehydrated by series of ethanol washes ( 30 % , 50 % , 70 % and 90 % ethanol for 5min each ) and further kept overnight under absolute ethanol . The dried sample was then imaged using the Multimode V AFM ( Veeco ) in tapping mode . 𝑇 . bhjh Supplemental mathematical modeling Here we describe our model for the determination of the shape of clathrin - coated buds as a function of the membrane’s mechanical parameters in the presence of a large ( non - limiting ) amount of the adaptor AP180 . We ﬁrst consider an inﬁnite , planar membrane with tension σ . The membrane is put in contact with a clathrin solution of ﬁxed concentration , which favors the assembly of clathrin buds of ﬁxed radius r c on the membrane . Consistent with the free energy per bud introduced in Eq . ( 1 ) of the main text , we write the free energy per unit surface area of the initially ﬂat membrane as f = σA m − µA c + τ‘ A p , ( S1 ) where µ = µ 0 − κ / ( 2 r 2 c ) is the free energy gain per unit area upon clathrin polymerization – here µ 0 is related to the concentration of the clathrin solution . In Eq . ( S1 ) , A m and A c respectively denote the membrane and coat areas associated with a single bud , ‘ is the length of the coat - bare membrane interface and A p is the projected area of the bud [ Fig . 1 ( a ) ] . We assume that our system is at equilibrium , implying that the system selects the bud shape that minimizes the free energy of Eq . ( S1 ) . In Sec . , we describe the various acceptable morphologies for the system and the associated free energies . In Sec . , we compare these energies and determine the one that dominates the system depending on its parameters . Finally , in Sec . , we describe the procedure used in the main text to extract these parameters from the experimental data . Bud geometry and energy We consider a membrane homogeneously covered by identical buds characterized by an opening angle θ [ Fig . 1 ( a ) ] . Buds may exhibit morphologies going from non - existent to partial to full buds , and in the fol - lowing sections we successively study the four subcases illustrated in Fig . 1 ( b ) . Bare membrane If clathrin does not bind to the membrane , we have A c = 0 and ‘ = 0 . The membrane is then ﬂat , implying A m = A p , and thus f bare σ = 1 . ( S2 ) Shallow partial bud We consider a bud with an opening angle θ ∈ [ 0 , π / 2 ] , implying A c = A m . Deﬁning the relative excess area as a = A m / A p − 1 , ( S3 ) it is easily shown that cos θ = 1 − a 1 + a , sin θ = 2 √ a 1 + a , r c ‘ A p = 1 + a √ a . ( S4 ) Deﬁning dimensionless variables ˜ µ = µ / σ and ˜ τ = τ / ( r c σ ) , ( S5 ) and minimizing the free energy over a we ﬁnd that for ˜ µ 6 1 f σ = ( 1 − ˜ µ ) ( 1 + a ) 2 1 − a , with a given by 1 − a 2 a 3 / 2 = 1 − ˜ µ ˜ τ ( S6 ) We plot the corresponding opening angle as a function of the dimensionless parameters in Fig . 1 ( c ) .
